Thus:

Therefore:

Now we can simplify p_y using relations between x0, y0, R, S, d. Let's try to express (x0 + R)(d + S - x0) in terms of something like (R^2 - x0^2) maybe. We have y0^2 = R^2 - x0^2. So we can get expressions.

Cancel p_y^2 both sides. Then:

Simplify the difference of squares:

(p_x - x0)^2 - (p_x + R)^2 = [ (p_x - x0) - (p_x + R) ] * [ (p_x - x0) + (p_x + R) ] = [ -x0 - R ] * [ 2p_x - x0 + R ] = -(x0 + R) * (2p_x - x0 + R).

Now we know p_x = (d + S - R)/2. So 2p_x = d + S - R. Then 2p_x - x0 + R = (d + S - R) - x0 + R = d + S - x0. So that's the same as before: (d + S - x0). So:

Let's try to compute explicitly using coordinates but also use symbolic algebra to simplify. Let's define:

r = R, s = S.

We have:

a = (1 + r^2 - s^2)/2.
h^2 = r^2 - a^2.

We can also express h^2 in terms of r,s:

h^2 = r^2 - ((1 + r^2 - s^2)^2)/4.

Simplify numerator:

4r^2 - (1 + r^2 - s^2)^2.

We can factor this as something like ( (s^2 - r^2) + (something) )? Actually it's a difference of squares: (2r)^2 - (1 + r^2 - s^2)^2 = (2r - (1 + r^2 - s^2)) * (2r + (1 + r^2 - s^2)). So:

h^2 = ( (2r - 1 - r^2 + s^2) * (2r + 1 + r^2 - s^2) ) / 4.

Let’s compute each factor:

F1 = 2r - 1 - r^2 + s^2 = s^2 - r^2 + 2r - 1.
F2 = 2r + 1 + r^2 - s^2 = (r^2 + 2r + 1) - s^2 = (r+1)^2 - s^2.

Thus:

h^2 = ( (s^2 - r^2 + 2r - 1) * ((r+1)^2 - s^2) ) / 4.

Now note that s^2 - r^2 + 2r - 1 = (s^2 - (r^2 - 2r + 1)) = s^2 - (r-1)^2. Because (r-1)^2 = r^2 -2r +1. So:

s^2 - r^2 + 2r - 1 = s^2 - (r^2 - 2r + 1) = s^2 - (r-1)^2.

Thus:

h^2 = ( (s^2 - (r-1)^2) * ((r+1)^2 - s^2) ) / 4.

Now we have a and h expressed. That's nice.

Now also compute p_x and p_y:

p_x = (1 + s - r)/2.

p_y = ( (r - 1 - s)(r + a) ) / (2 h). Since r + a = r + (1 + r^2 - s^2)/2 = (2r + 1 + r^2 - s^2)/2 = ((r+1)^2 - s^2)/2.

So r + a = ((r+1)^2 - s^2)/2. Then:

p_y = ( (r - 1 - s) * ((r+1)^2 - s^2)/2 ) / (2 h) = ( (r - 1 - s) * ((r+1)^2 - s^2) ) / (4 h).

Now note that (r - 1 - s) = -(s + 1 - r). So:

p_y = - (s + 1 - r) * ((r+1)^2 - s^2) / (4 h).

Thus p_y is negative if ((r+1)^2 - s^2) > 0, which is true because s^2 < (r+1)^2? Actually from intersection condition s - r < 1, but that doesn't guarantee (r+1)^2 > s^2. Let's see: s^2 < (r+1)^2 is equivalent to s < r+1, which is the same as s - r < 1. Indeed s - r < 1 ⇒ s < r+1. So (r+1)^2 - s^2 > 0. So that factor is positive. Also (s+1 - r) > 0 because s - r < 1 ⇒ s+1 - r > 0. So p_y is negative.

Thus p_y < 0. So P is below MN? Actually y-coordinate is negative, so P is below MN. But A is above MN (y0 > 0). So AP crosses MN at some point (maybe between). That's okay.

Now we can compute P (circumcenter of triangle ACD). Coordinates:

A = (0, 1)

Now p_y: We have (p_x - 0)^2 + (p_y - 1)^2 = (p_x - (u - R))^2 + (p_y - 0)^2. That's one equation. Solve for p_y. Let's do that.

Let’s denote:

C_x = u - R.
D_x = v + S.

Then p_x = (C_x + D_x)/2.

We have:

(p_x)^2 + (p_y - 1)^2 = (p_x - C_x)^2 + p_y^2.

Expand:

p_x^2 + (p_y^2 - 2p_y + 1) = (p_x^2 - 2p_x C_x + C_x^2) + p_y^2.

Cancel p_y^2 both sides. Then:

p_x^2 - 2p_y + 1 = p_x^2 - 2p_x C_x + C_x^2.

Cancel p_x^2:

-2p_y + 1 = -2p_x C_x + C_x^2.

Thus:

-2p_y = -2p_x C_x + C_x^2 - 1.

Multiply by -1: 2p_y = 2p_x C_x - C_x^2 + 1.

So p_y = p_x C_x - (C_x^2)/2 + 1/2.

That's an expression. But we also have that P is equidistant to A and D, which would give another expression, but they should be consistent. But we can also derive using A and D, but it's okay.

Then y = t (v - p_x) = (p_x - u)(v - p_x) / p_y.

Thus H = (p_x, (p_x - u)(v - p_x) / p_y). That's correct.

Now we need to prove that L: line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circle (BEF). B = (0, -1). E and F are second intersections of AP with Ω and Γ. Since A = (0,1). So AP is a line from (0,1) to P. We can compute E and F using the midpoint method as before. Let's do that.

We have A = (0,1). P = (p_x, p_y). The line AP is param: A + λ v, where v = P - A = (p_x, p_y - 1). Then the foot from M (u,0) to AP is the midpoint of AE. Similarly, foot from N (v,0) is midpoint of AF. So we can compute:

Let v = (v_x, v_y) = (p_x, p_y - 1). Then for M: we need the orthogonal projection of M onto line AP. But careful: The foot from M to AP is the point X_M on AP such that (X_M - M) ⟂ v. Then X_M is midpoint of AE. So we can compute X_M = A + t_M v, where t_M = - ( (M - A) · v ) / |v|^2? Let's derive.

We have line AP: A + λ v. For a point M, the foot is given by λ = - ( (M - A) · v ) / |v|^2. Actually the vector from A to foot is the projection of (M - A) onto v. The foot is A + proj_v (M - A). So λ = ((M - A) · v) / |v|^2. That gives the foot. But we need to confirm: For foot from M to line, we want (A + λ v) - M to be perpendicular to v. That gives (A - M + λ v) · v = 0 → (A - M)·v + λ |v|^2 = 0 → λ = - (A - M)·v / |v|^2 = (M - A)·v / |v|^2. So indeed λ = ((M - A) · v) / |v|^2. So foot_M = A + λ v. That's the projection of M onto line AP. However is that the midpoint of AE? Let's verify.

We have A and E are points on Ω (circle centered at M) and the line AP is the line. The chord AE of Ω lies on AP. The midpoint of AE is the foot of the perpendicular from M to AP. Indeed, for a circle, the line from the center perpendicular to a chord bisects the chord. So the perpendicular from M to AP meets AP at the midpoint of AE. So that foot is exactly the midpoint. So indeed, X_M = foot_M is the midpoint of AE. So we can compute E = 2 X_M - A = A + 2 λ v.

Similarly, for N: foot_N = A + μ v, where μ = ((N - A) · v) / |v|^2. Then F = A + 2 μ v.

Thus we have:

E = A + 2 λ v, F = A + 2 μ v, with λ, μ as above.

Now, note that A is (0,1). So these are expressed.

(p_x)^2 + (p_y - h)^2 = (p_x - (m - R))^2 + (p_y - 0)^2.

Simplify:

2p_y h = 2p_x(m - R) - (m - R)^2 + h^2.

Now, let's adopt this coordinate system:

The midpoint of AB is (0, b/2). So MN passes through (0, b/2) and is horizontal. Thus, M and N lie on the line y = b/2. So we set:

M = (m, b/2). N = (n, b/2). Since circles intersect, we have |M A| = R, |N A| = S. So:

m^2 + (b/2)^2 = R^2. (1)
n^2 + (b/2)^2 = S^2. (2)

Also, A and B are distinct, so b ≠ 0.

Now, the ordering C, M, N, D along MN: C is on Ω, left of M, so C = (m - R, b/2). D is on Γ, right of N, so D = (n + S, b/2). The condition m - R < m < n < n + S.

Now, P is circumcenter of triangle ACD. A = (0,0), C = (m - R, b/2), D = (n + S, b/2). So triangle with A at origin. That's nice. Then the circumcenter P is determined. Since A is origin, we can compute P using coordinates.

Now, H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. M and N are on y = b/2. So MN is horizontal. So altitude from P is vertical. So H lies on vertical line through P. That's similar.

Now, the line AP is the line through A and P, which is just the line through origin and P. So direction vector is P. So L is line through H parallel to P. So L is a line with direction P, passing through H.

Now, E is second intersection of AP (line through origin and P) with Ω. Since A is origin and is on Ω, the line passes through A, so E is the other intersection. Similarly, F is second intersection of AP with Γ.

Now, B is (0,b). So we need to prove that the line through H parallel to P is tangent to circumcircle of BEF.

This seems a bit more manageable because A=0 simplifies many expressions.

Now, let's compute P. A = (0,0). C = (c, h) where c = m - R, h = b/2. D = (d, h) where d = n + S. So C and D have same y-coordinate h. So CD is horizontal. The perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical line x = (c + d)/2. So P lies on that vertical line. Also, P lies on perpendicular bisector of AC (since it's circumcenter). So we can compute P coordinates.

Let’s denote:

A = (0,0)
C = (C_x, C_y) = (m - R, h)
D = (D_x, D_y) = (n + S, h)

We have h = b/2.

Now, we can compute P as intersection of perpendicular bisectors. Let's derive formulas.

Since A is origin, the perpendicular bisector of AC is the set of points X such that |X - A| = |X - C| → |X|^2 = |X - C|^2. That gives:

X·X = (X - C)·(X - C) = X·X - 2 X·C + C·C → cancel X·X → 0 = -2 X·C + C·C → X·C = C·C / 2.

Thus, the perpendicular bisector of AC is the line (in 2D) given by X·C = |C|^2 / 2. That's a line (since C is a vector). Similarly, the perpendicular bisector of AD is X·D = |D|^2 / 2. The circumcenter P is the intersection of these two lines. Also, P must satisfy that P lies on perpendicular bisector of CD, which is x = (C_x + D_x)/2. However solving X·C = |C|^2/2 and X·D = |D|^2/2 is a linear system in X (two equations). That's nice. So we can solve for P.

Thus, we have:

P·C = (|C|^2)/2. (1)
P·D = (|D|^2)/2. (2)

Additionally, we know that C and D have the same y-coordinate. So we can compute P.

Now, we also have that A is at origin, so AP is just the vector P. So the direction of AP is P. So L is line through H parallel to P.

Now, H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. M = (m, h), N = (n, h). So MN is horizontal. So altitude from P is vertical. So H has x-coordinate equal to P_x. So H = (P_x, y_H). The altitude from M is perpendicular to PN. So we can compute y_H as before.

Now, we also need to compute E and F. Since A=0, line AP is the line through origin with direction P. So param: t P. Then E is the second intersection with Ω. Ω is circle centered at M with radius R. Since A is on Ω, we have |M| = R. The line through origin (A) with direction P intersects Ω at A (t=0) and another point E = t_E P, where t_E is determined by |t_E P - M|^2 = R^2. Since A is on Ω, we have |M|^2 = R^2. So:

|t P - M|^2 = R^2 → |t P|^2 - 2 t P·M + |M|^2 = R^2 → t^2 |P|^2 - 2 t (P·M) + R^2 = R^2 → t^2 |P|^2 - 2 t (P·M) = 0 → t (t |P|^2 - 2 (P·M)) = 0. Thus, t=0 (A) or t = 2 (P·M) / |P|^2. So E = (2 (P·M) / |P|^2) P.

Similarly, F is the second intersection with Γ, center N, radius S. Since A is on Γ, we have |N|^2 = S^2. Then |t P - N|^2 = S^2 → t^2 |P|^2 - 2 t (P·N) + S^2 = S^2 → t^2 |P|^2 - 2 t (P·N) = 0 → t = 0 or t = 2 (P·N) / |P|^2. So F = (2 (P·N) / |P|^2) P.

Thus, E and F are scalar multiples of P. That's nice.

Now, B = (0, b). So triangle BEF: B, E = α P, F = β P, where α = 2 (P·M) / |P|^2, β = 2 (P·N) / |P|^2.

Now, the circumcircle of BEF. Since E and F are collinear with origin, the line EF is the same as AP (through origin). So EF is a line through origin. B is a point not on that line generally. So the circle (BEF) is determined by B and the two points on a line through origin.

Now, the line L is through H parallel to P. So L is a line with direction P, passing through H.

We need to prove that L is tangent to circle (BEF). Perhaps we can find that the circle (BEF) is the circle with diameter something like the segment connecting the projections? But now we have a parametric expression.

Now, let's compute P in terms of m, n, b (and R,S). Since we have expressions for C and D.

We have:

C = (m - R, h) with h = b/2. D = (n + S, h). So C_x = m - R, D_x = n + S. Also, C_y = D_y = h.

Thus, C = (c, h), D = (d, h). Then:

|C|^2 = (m - R)^2 + h^2.
|D|^2 = (n + S)^2 + h^2.

Now, we also have R^2 = m^2 + h^2, S^2 = n^2 + h^2. So we can simplify expressions.

Let's compute C and D using these relations. Maybe we can find P as something like (some vector). Since we have linear equations:

P·C = |C|^2 / 2.
P·D = |D|^2 / 2.

This is a 2x2 linear system. Solve for P.

We can also note that P is the circumcenter, so it's the intersection of perpendicular bisectors. There might be a direct formula: P = (|C|^2 D - |D|^2 C) / (2 (C × D))? That's for the circumcenter in barycentric? Actually in vector form, the circumcenter of triangle with vertices A,B,C (A=0) can be expressed as (|B|^2 C - |C|^2 B) / (2 (B × C))? That's for 2D? That seems like something for the circumcenter of a triangle with one vertex at origin, using the fact that the perpendicular bisectors are lines. But we have two equations: P·C = |C|^2/2, P·D = |D|^2/2. That's a linear system. If we treat C and D as vectors, then we can solve for P as a linear combination of C and D? Actually we can represent P in the basis {C, D}. Since C and D are likely not parallel (they are not, because they have same y but different x, so they are not parallel unless they are the same vector). So they are linearly independent (unless one is a multiple of the other, which would happen if C and D are collinear with origin? That's not likely). So we can solve.

Write P = u C + v D. Then compute P·C = u |C|^2 + v (C·D). Similarly, P·D = u (C·D) + v |D|^2. This gives two equations:

u |C|^2 + v (C·D) = |C|^2 / 2.
u (C·D) + v |D|^2 = |D|^2 / 2.

We can solve for u and v. This seems doable.

Alternatively, we can directly compute P by solving the two linear equations. Since C and D are known, we can treat P as unknown (x,y) and solve:

C_x x + C_y y = (C_x^2 + C_y^2) / 2.
D_x x + D_y y = (D_x^2 + D_y^2) / 2.

That's a 2x2 linear system. That seems easier.

Let's denote:

C = (c1, c2) = (m - R, h).
D = (d1, d2) = (n + S, h).

Then:

c1 x + c2 y = (c1^2 + c2^2) / 2.   (1)
d1 x + d2 y = (d1^2 + d2^2) / 2.   (2)

We can solve for x,y.

Now, note that c2 = d2 = h. So the system is:

c1 x + h y = (c1^2 + h^2) / 2.
d1 x + h y = (d1^2 + h^2) / 2.

Subtract: (c1 - d1) x = (c1^2 - d1^2) / 2 = (c1 - d1)(c1 + d1) / 2. Since c1 - d1 ≠ 0 (likely), we get:

x = (c1 + d1) / 2.

Thus, P_x = (c1 + d1) / 2. That's the same as the midpoint of CD's x-coordinate, which we already knew because P lies on perpendicular bisector of CD (vertical). So that's consistent.

Now, we can plug x into one equation to find y:

c1 * ((c1 + d1)/2) + h y = (c1^2 + h^2) / 2 → (c1 (c1 + d1) ) / 2 + h y = (c1^2 + h^2) / 2 → multiply by 2: c1 (c1 + d1) + 2 h y = c1^2 + h^2 → c1^2 + c1 d1 + 2 h y = c1^2 + h^2 → cancel c1^2 → c1 d1 + 2 h y = h^2 → 2 h y = h^2 - c1 d1 → y = (h^2 - c1 d1) / (2 h).

Thus, P = ( (c1 + d1)/2, (h^2 - c1 d1) / (2 h) ).

Now, c1 = m - R, d1 = n + S.

Now, we also have expressions for R^2 = m^2 + h^2, S^2 = n^2 + h^2.

Now, we can compute P·M and P·N needed for α and β.

We have M = (m, h), N = (n, h). P is known. So we can compute P·M = P_x * m + P_y * h.

Similarly, P·N = P_x * n + P_y * h.

Now, we also need |P|^2.

This looks like a good path.

Now, we also need H, the orthocenter of triangle PMN. We have M and N on y = h. So MN is horizontal. So altitude from P is vertical. So H has x-coordinate equal to P_x. So H = (P_x, y_H). To find y_H, we can use altitude from M: line through M perpendicular to PN. Vector PN = N - P = (n - P_x, h - P_y). So direction perpendicular to PN is (h - P_y, n - P_x) or something. But perhaps we can derive a formula for y_H in terms of P, M, N. Since H is orthocenter, we have that H lies on the vertical line through P, and also on the line through M that is perpendicular to PN. That's okay.

Alternatively, we can compute H using the relation H = P + M + N - 2 * O, where O is circumcenter of PMN. But maybe easier to use altitudes. Since MN is horizontal, the altitude from P is vertical. So H's x-coordinate is P_x. The altitude from M is perpendicular to PN. So we need to find y such that point (P_x, y) lies on line through M with direction perpendicular to PN. So we can solve.

Let's compute H_y using that.

Given M = (m, h), N = (n, h). P = (p_x, p_y). Then:

PN = (n - p_x, h - p_y). A vector perpendicular to PN is (h - p_y, -(n - p_x)) or (h - p_y, p_x - n). Actually dot product: (n - p_x)*(h - p_y) + (h - p_y)*(p_x - n) = (n - p_x)*(h - p_y) - (n - p_x)*(h - p_y) = 0. So (h - p_y, p_x - n) is perpendicular. Also, (h - p_y, -(n - p_x)) works.

So the altitude from M has direction vector v_M = (h - p_y, p_x - n). Its param: (m, h) + t * (h - p_y, p_x - n). Intersection with x = p_x gives:

m + t (h - p_y) = p_x → t = (p_x - m) / (h - p_y), provided h ≠ p_y. Then y = h + t (p_x - n). So y_H = h + (p_x - m)(p_x - n) / (h - p_y). That's an expression.

But earlier we had a simpler expression when M was at (0,0) etc. This is messy. However note that we have p_y expressed in terms of h, m, R, S, etc. So maybe simplifications occur. But perhaps we can find H more directly using the fact that in any triangle, the reflection of the orthocenter over a side lies on the circumcircle. Since we have an orthocenter, we can also use the formula: H = A + B + C - 2 * O (circumcenter). But we don't know O.

But maybe we don't need explicit H. Perhaps we can prove that L is tangent to (BEF) by showing that the distance from the center of (BEF) to L equals the radius, and that the center of (BEF) is something like the midpoint of BH or something that can be expressed nicely. Let's compute the circle (BEF) using coordinates with A=0.

We have:

B = (0, b). E = α P. F = β P. P is known. So E and F are on the line through origin with direction P. So the line L (through H parallel to P) is parallel to that line. So L is a line parallel to the line containing E,F. So we have a triangle with base EF on that line, and vertex B off that line. The circumcircle of BEF has its center somewhere on the perpendicular bisector of EF. That perpendicular bisector is perpendicular to P (since EF is collinear with P). So the center O lies on a line perpendicular to P. Also, O lies on perpendicular bisector of B and E. That seems messy, but perhaps we can find that O is the midpoint of BH? Let's test numeric with this coordinate system.

Set A = (0,0). Choose b = 2 (so AB length 2). Then h = b/2 = 1. So MN is y = 1. Choose m = 0.5, n = 2 (ensuring u < v). Then compute R^2 = 0.5^2 + 1 = 1.25 → R ≈ 1.118. S^2 = 4 + 1 = 5 → S ≈ 2.236. Then C = (m - R, h) = (0.5 - 1.118, 1) = (-0.618, 1). D = (n + S, h) = (2 + 2.236, 1) = (4.236, 1). Then p_x = (c1 + d1)/2 = (-0.618 + 4.236)/2 = 3.618/2 = 1.809. p_y = (h^2 - c1 d1) / (2h) = (1 - (-0.618*4.236)) / 2 = (1 + 2.618) / 2 = 3.618/2 = 1.809. So P = (1.809, 1.809). That's interesting: P_y = p_y = 1.809, which is same as p_x? Actually p_x ≈ 1.809, p_y ≈ 1.809. So P is on the line y = x. That's plausible because we derived y = (h^2 - c1 d1)/(2h). With h=1, c1 d1 = (-0.618)*(4.236) ≈ -2.618, so h^2 - c1 d1 = 1 - (-2.618) = 3.618, /2 = 1.809. So indeed p_y = 1.809. So P = (1.809, 1.809). So P lies on line y = x.

Now, M = (0.5, 1). N = (2, 1). Compute α = 2 (P·M) / |P|^2. P·M = 1.809*0.5 + 1.809*1 = 0.9045 + 1.809 = 2.7135. |P|^2 = 1.809^2 + 1.809^2 = 2 * 3.273 = 6.546. So α = 2 * 2.7135 / 6.546 = 5.427 / 6.546 ≈ 0.8295. Then E = α P ≈ (1.809*0.8295, 1.809*0.8295) = (1.5, 1.5) approximately. Indeed earlier with A=0 we got E ≈ (1.5,1.5). That's consistent.

β = 2 (P·N) / |P|^2. P·N = 1.809*2 + 1.809*1 = 3.618 + 1.809 = 5.427. β = 2 * 5.427 / 6.546 = 10.854 / 6.546 ≈ 1.658. Then F = β P ≈ (1.809*1.658, 1.809*1.658) ≈ (3.0, 3.0) approximately (3.0, 3.0). So F ≈ (3,3). That's interesting.

Now, B = (0,2). So triangle BEF: B(0,2), E(1.5,1.5), F(3,3). The circumcenter O of BEF? We can compute quickly. But we already have L: line through H parallel to P. H is orthocenter of PMN. Let's compute H.

M(0.5,1), N(2,1), P(1.809,1.809). MN horizontal. So altitude from P is vertical x = 1.809. So H_x = 1.809. Now, compute H_y using altitude from M. We have PN = N - P = (2 - 1.809, 1 - 1.809) = (0.191, -0.809). A direction perpendicular to PN is ( -0.809, -0.191) or (0.809, 0.191). The altitude from M is line through M with direction perpendicular to PN. So we need to find y such that (1.809, y) lies on that line.

Alternatively, compute H using formula H = P + M + N - 2 * O? That's messy.

Let's compute H_y via solving the system: H = (p_x, y). Since H is orthocenter, we have that MH ⟂ PN. So vector (H - M) is perpendicular to PN. So (H - M)·PN = 0. H - M = (p_x - m, y - h). PN = (n - p_x, h - p_y). So:

(p_x - m)*(n - p_x) + (y - h)*(h - p_y) = 0.

We can solve for y. That might be simpler.

We have p_x = 1.809, m = 0.5, n = 2, h = 1, p_y = 1.809. So:

(p_x - m) = 1.309, (n - p_x) = 0.191. (y - h) = (y - 1). (h - p_y) = -0.809. So equation:

1.309 * 0.191 + (y - 1) * (-0.809) = 0 → 0.25 - 0.809(y - 1) = 0 → 0.809(y - 1) = 0.25 → y - 1 = 0.309 → y ≈ 1.309. So H ≈ (1.809, 1.309). That's interesting.

Now, L is line through H parallel to P, which is direction (1.809, 1.809). So L is line with slope 1 passing through (1.809, 1.309). So its equation: y - 1.309 = 1 * (x - 1.809). That's y = x - 0.5. So L is the line y = x - 0.5.

Now, the circle (BEF) we can compute its center and radius to see if L is tangent. Let's compute the circumcircle of B(0,2), E(1.5,1.5), F(3,3). We can compute its equation. Because E and F are on line y = x (since (1.5,1.5) and (3,3) satisfy y = x). B is (0,2). So the triangle is not isosceles but we can compute.

We can compute the circle's center O. Let's find perpendicular bisector of EF. EF is on y = x. Midpoint of EF is ((1.5+3)/2 = 2.25, (1.5+3)/2 = 2.25). So midpoint is (2.25,2.25). The perpendicular bisector of EF is the line perpendicular to y = x, i.e., slope -1, passing through (2.25,2.25). That's line: y - 2.25 = -1 (x - 2.25) → y = -x + 4.5. That's one line.

Now, perpendicular bisector of BE. B(0,2), E(1.5,1.5). Midpoint: (0.75, 1.75). Slope of BE: (1.5-2)/(1.5-0) = (-0.5)/1.5 = -1/3. So perpendicular slope = 3. Equation: y - 1.75 = 3 (x - 0.75). That's y = 3x - 2.25 + 1.75 = 3x - 0.5. So we solve with y = -x + 4.5.

Set -x + 4.5 = 3x - 0.5 → 4.5 + 0.5 = 3x + x → 5 = 4x → x = 1.25. Then y = -1.25 + 4.5 = 3.25. So O = (1.25, 3.25). That's center. Radius squared: distance from O to B: (0-1.25)^2 + (2-3.25)^2 = 1.5625 + 1.5625 = 3.125. So radius = sqrt(3.125) ≈ 1.768. Now, L is line y = x - 0.5. Distance from O(1.25,3.25) to L: line in form x - y - 0.5 = 0? Actually y = x - 0.5 → x - y - 0.5 = 0. Distance = |1.25 - 3.25 - 0.5| / sqrt(1^2 + (-1)^2) = | -2.5| / sqrt(2) = 2.5 / 1.414 ≈ 1.768. That's exactly the radius. So L is tangent. Great! So the analytic approach works with this coordinate system.

But we saw that O = (1.25, 3.25). That's interesting. Maybe O is the midpoint of something like the segment connecting the projections? Not.

We can try to derive O in terms of P and B. Since E and F are scalar multiples of P, the line EF is the line through origin in direction P. So points E and F are collinear with origin. The circumcircle of BEF is determined by B and the two points on that line. There is a known formula: For two points on a line through origin, say E = t1 P, F = t2 P, and a point B not on that line, the circle passing through B, E, F has its center at the intersection of the perpendicular bisector of EF (which is a line perpendicular to P) and the perpendicular bisector of BE. Given the numeric success, we can attempt to prove that L is tangent to (BEF) by showing that the distance from the center O to L equals the radius. We can compute O explicitly using vector algebra.

Let's adopt the coordinate system with A=0. Then:

B = (0, b). h = b/2.

c1 = m - R

Now, we need to simplify expressions. Since R^2 = m^2 + h^2, S^2 = n^2 + h^2. So we can find rational expressions for R and S? But they appear linearly. (n + S) p_x + h p_y = ((n + S)^2 + h^2) / 2.   (2)

Subtract: ((m - R) - (n + S)) p_x = ( (m - R)^2 - (n + S)^2 ) / 2. The left coefficient is (m - n - R - S). That's messy. Better approach: Since we have a parametric description, we can compute the power of a point on L with respect to circle (BEF) and show that L is tangent because the distance from the center to L equals the radius. But we need an expression for the center O of (BEF). That seems plausible to compute by solving linear equations from equal distances. Let's attempt to do that.

Let’s denote:

Let’s define vectors:

Let B = (0, b). Let P be a vector (p_x, p_y). Then E = α P, F = β P, with α, β as above.

We want to find the circumcenter O of triangle BEF. Since B is not collinear with P, we can find O.

We have three points. Let’s denote O = (x, y). Then:

|O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2 = |O - F|^2.

We have:

|O - E|^2 = |O|^2 - 2 O·E + |E|^2.
|O - F|^2 = |O|^2 - 2 O·F + |F|^2.
|O - B|^2 = |O|^2 - 2 O·B + |B|^2.

Equality |O - E|^2 = |O - F|^2 yields:

-2 O·E + |E|^2 = -2 O·F + |F|^2 → 2 O·(F - E) = |F|^2 - |E|^2.

Since E and F are multiples of P, we have F - E = (β - α) P. So this is a linear equation in O. Similarly, equality |O - E|^2 = |O - B|^2 yields:

-2 O·E + |E|^2 = -2 O·B + |B|^2 → 2 O·(B - E) = |B|^2 - |E|^2.

So we have two linear equations in O. That's a system. Solve for O. That seems manageable. Then we can compute the distance from O to L (line through H parallel to P). L is defined by H + t P. So we need to show that the distance from O to L is equal to the radius (which is |O - B|). Alternatively, we can try to prove that L is the polar of something, but this is direct.

O·(B - α v) = 2 - 2 (M·v)^2 / |v|^2.   (2)

We want to express L = -v2 x + v1 y as a linear combination of these. Let’s find coefficients p,q such that:

p (v1 x + v2 y) + q ( -B v1 x + (A - B v2) y ) = -v2 x + v1 y.

Then:

x coefficient: p v1 + q (-B v1) = v1 (p - q B) must equal -v2.
y coefficient: p v2 + q (A - B v2) must equal v1.

Thus:

v1 (p - q B) = -v2.   (I)
p v2 + q (A - B v2) = v1.   (II)

We can try to solve for p,q. This seems messy. However maybe we can find a direct expression for b using the fact that O is the circumcenter of BEF. There might be a synthetic relation: Since E and F are on line v, the perpendicular bisector of EF is perpendicular to v. The circumcenter O lies on that line. Also, the perpendicular bisector of BE is some line. Maybe we can find O as the intersection of the line with direction w (perpendicular) and the line that is the reflection of something. But perhaps we can compute b more directly by noting that O is also the center of the circle passing through B, and the line through O perpendicular to v is the line that is the radical axis of something? Not.

Given the difficulty, maybe we can adopt a different coordinate system: Place MN as the x-axis. Then M and N are on x-axis. Let A and B be points with same x-coordinate (say a). Then A = (a, h), B = (a, -h). Then the line MN is horizontal (x-axis). The points C and D are on the x-axis. That's similar to before but with A and B not at origin. Let's step back and consider the possibility of a synthetic proof. I recall a known geometry problem: "Let Ω and Γ be two intersecting circles with centers M and N. Let AB be their common chord. Let C be the point where Ω meets line MN on the opposite side of M from N, and D be the point where Γ meets line MN on the opposite side of N from M. Let P be the circumcenter of triangle ACD. Let H be the orthocenter of triangle PMN. Prove that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to the circumcircle of triangle BEF, where E = AP ∩ Ω (other than A), F = AP ∩ Γ (other than A)." That's exactly the problem. So it's a known configuration. The analytic solution is plausible, but we need to present it clearly.

Let's try to solve analytically with A=0, b=2, h=1. We'll compute P and then O. Then we'll compute distance from O to L. We'll also compute radius. That should simplify.

We have:

v = (v1, v2) = P.
M = (m, 1), N = (n, 1).

We have:

R^2 = m^2+1, S^2 = n^2+1.

c1 = m - R, d1 = n + S.
p_x = (c1+d1)/2.
p_y = (1 - c1 d1)/2.

Now, we also have that α = 2 (M·v) / |v|^2 = 2 (m v1 + v2) / A.
Similarly, β = 2 (n v1 + v2) / A.

Now, we derived that (α+β)/2 = ( (m+n) v1 + 2 v2 ) / A. That's a = (α+β)/2. So a A = (m+n) v1 + 2 v2.

Now, Eq1: v1 x + v2 y = a A.

But we also have from earlier that (α+β)/2 * |v|^2 = (m+n) v1 + 2 v2. That's a A. So indeed, Eq1 is v·O = a A.

Now, Eq2 from B and E: O·(B - α v) = (4 - α^2 A)/2 = 2 - 2 (M·v)^2 / A. That's fine.

Now, we can solve O. Let's do it systematically.

Let’s denote:

v = (v1, v2). A = v1^2+v2^2. B = M·v = m v1 + v2.

We have U = v·O = a A = (m+n) v1 + 2 v2. (1)

We also have V = O·(B - α v) = 2 - 2 B^2 / A. (2)

But we can also derive a linear equation for O in a different way: from equality |O - E|^2 = |O - B|^2, we got:

2 O·(B - E) = |B|^2 - |E|^2. That's what we used. That's linear.

Now, perhaps we can find O by solving two linear equations, but we already have a system. However note that we derived a formula for y: y = (V + B U) / A. That's the y-coordinate of O. So we have O = (x, y) with y known. Then we can compute O·w = b = ( -v2 x + v1 y ) / |v|.

We can also compute x from the system, but maybe we can avoid x by using a trick: The distance from O to L is | (O - H)·w |. H is on x = v1. So H = (v1, y_H). Then (O - H)·w = (O·w) - (H·w). So we need H·w. Compute H·w. Since w = (-v2, v1)/|v|, H·w = ( -v2 * v1 + v1 * y_H ) / |v| = ( -v1 v2 + v1 y_H ) / |v| = v1 ( y_H - v2 ) / |v|.

Thus, (O - H)·w = b - v1 ( y_H - v2 ) / |v|.

Now, b = ( -v2 x + v1 y ) / |v|. So we can compute b.

Maybe we can find that y_H is such that v1 ( y_H - v2 ) / |v| = something like b? That would make distance zero, not. So we need to compute.

But perhaps we can compute O·w directly from the linear system using Cramer's rule for the perpendicular component. Since we have two equations, we can solve for O in terms of u and w. Let's do that.

Let’s set up basis: Let e1 = v / |v| (unit parallel), e2 = w (unit perpendicular). Then O = a e1 + b e2, where a = (v·O)/|v| = U / |v|. Then b = O·w. We can also compute b by taking dot product of the second equation with w. Because the second equation is O·(B - α v) = RHS. So:

O·(B - α v) = (a e1 + b e2)·(B - α v) = a e1·(B - α v) + b e2·(B - α v). Since e1·v = |v|, e2·v = 0. So e1·(B - α v) = e1·B - α |v|. e2·(B - α v) = e2·B - α e2·v = e2·B.

Thus:

O·(B - α v) = a (B·e1 - α |v|) + b (B·e2). (Equation A)

Now, we also have a = U / |v|, and U is known. So we can solve for b.

Now, B·e1 = (0,2)·v / |v| = 2 v2 / |v|. B·e2 = (0,2)·w = 2 v1 / |v|. So:

a (B·e1 - α |v|) = (U / |v|) * (2 v2 / |v| - α |v|) = U * (2 v2 - α |v|^2) / |v|^2.

Thus, equation becomes:

U * (2 v2 - α A) / A + b * (2 v1 / |v|) = RHS.   (1)

But we can also compute α A = 2 (M·v) = 2 B. So α A = 2 B. So:

U * (2 v2 - 2 B) / A = 2 U (v2 - B) / A.

Now, U = a A = (m+n) v1 + 2 v2. So that's known. Also, B = m v1 + v2. So v2 - B = v2 - (m v1 + v2) = -m v1. So (v2 - B) = -m v1. Then U * (v2 - B) = U * (-m v1) = -m v1 U. So:

2 U (v2 - B) / A = -2 m v1 U / A.

Thus, the term becomes:

U * (2 v2 - α A) / A = -2 m v1 U / A.

Now, RHS = 2 - 2 B^2 / A.

So we have:

-2 m v1 U / A + b * (2 v1 / |v|) = 2 - 2 B^2 / A.

Multiply by A: -2 m v1 U + b * (2 v1 A / |v|) = 2A - 2 B^2.

Divide by 2: - m v1 U + b * (v1 A / |v|) = A - B^2.

Now, note that v1 A / |v| = v1 * |v|. Because A = |v|^2, so A / |v| = |v|. So:

b * (v1 |v|) = A - B^2 + m v1 U.

Thus,

b = (A - B^2 + m v1 U) / (v1 |v|).   (Equation for b)

Now, U = (m+n) v1 + 2 v2. So that's okay.

Now, we also need H·w. H = (v1, y_H). So H·w = ( -v2 * v1 + v1 * y_H ) / |v| = v1 ( y_H - v2 ) / |v|.

We can compute y_H from orthocenter. Let's derive y_H.

We have triangle PMN. M = (m,1), N = (n,1). P = (v1, v2). H lies on x = v1. So H = (v1, y_H). Orthocenter condition: (H - M) · (N - P) = 0. That's:

(v1 - m, y_H - 1) · (n - v1, 1 - v2) = 0.

Thus:

(v1 - m)*(n - v1) + (y_H - 1)*(1 - v2) = 0 → (y_H - 1)*(1 - v2) = - (v1 - m)*(n - v1). So:

y_H - 1 = - (v1 - m)*(n - v1) / (1 - v2), provided v2 ≠ 1. If v2 = 1, then P is on the line y = 1 (i.e., on MN), which would be degenerate? But P could be on MN? Let's check. P is circumcenter of ACD. Could it be on MN? Possibly if A, C, D are such that P lies on MN. But earlier we argued that P is on perpendicular bisector of CD, which is vertical. MN is horizontal, so P is not on MN unless it's at the intersection (which is the midpoint of CD). That point lies on MN? The midpoint of CD has y-coordinate 1 (since C and D have y=1). So its y-coordinate is 1. The x-coordinate is p_x. So that point lies on MN? MN is the line y = 1, yes. So P_x (p_x) is the x-coordinate of the midpoint, which lies on MN (y=1). But P is (p_x, p_y). p_y is not necessarily 1. So P is not on MN (unless p_y = 1). But is it possible that p_y = 1? That would make P on MN. Let's see if that can happen. With b=2, h=1, p_y = (1 - (m - R)(n + S))/2. That could be 1. That would imply (1 - (m - R)(n + S))/2 = 1 → 1 - (m - R)(n + S) = 2 → -(m - R)(n + S) = 1 → (m - R)(n + S) = -1. That seems plausible. So P could be on MN. In that case, v2 = 1. Then the altitude from P is vertical, and H lies on that vertical line. But also, H is orthocenter, but if P is on MN, then triangle PMN has a right angle at something? Actually if P is on MN, then P is collinear with M and N, making triangle degenerate? No, P is on the line MN? If v2 = 1, then P lies on y = 1, which is MN. So P is on MN. Then triangle PMN has all three points on the same line? That would be degenerate. But M and N are on y = 1. If P is also on y = 1, then they are collinear. The problem states that H is orthocenter of triangle PMN, which would be undefined if triangle is degenerate. Therefore, we must have that triangle PMN is non-degenerate. This implies that P is not on the line MN. So v2 ≠ 1. Similarly, we should check that P is not on MN. Indeed, the problem defines H as orthocentre of triangle PMN, so triangle PMN is non-degenerate. That means P is not on MN. So v2 ≠ 1. That's consistent with our earlier argument that P is not on MN. So we can assume v2 ≠ 1. Good.

Thus, we can compute y_H as:

y_H = 1 - (v1 - m)*(n - v1) / (1 - v2). That's okay.

Now, H·w = v1 ( y_H - v2 ) / |v|. So:

H·w = v1 ( 1 - (v1 - m)*(n - v1) / (1 - v2) - v2 ) / |v| = v1 ( 1 - v2 - (v1 - m)*(n - v1) / (1 - v2) ) / |v|.

Simplify.

Now, b = (A - B^2 + m v1 U) / (v1 |v|). Then (O - H)·w = b - H·w.

We want to show that |(O - H)·w| = |O - B|.

Let's try to compute explicitly using coordinates but also use symbolic algebra to simplify. Let's define:

r = R, s = S.

We have:

a = (1 + r^2 - s^2)/2.
h^2 = r^2 - a^2.

We can also express h^2 in terms of r,s:

h^2 = r^2 - ((1 + r^2 - s^2)^2)/4.

Simplify numerator:

4r^2 - (1 + r^2 - s^2)^2.

We can factor this as something like ( (s^2 - r^2) + (something) )? Actually it's a difference of squares: (2r)^2 - (1 + r^2 - s^2)^2 = (2r - (1 + r^2 - s^2)) * (2r + (1 + r^2 - s^2)). So:

h^2 = ( (2r - 1 - r^2 + s^2) * (2r + 1 + r^2 - s^2) ) / 4.

Let’s compute each factor:

F1 = 2r - 1 - r^2 + s^2 = s^2 - r^2 + 2r - 1.
F2 = 2r + 1 + r^2 - s^2 = (r^2 + 2r + 1) - s^2 = (r+1)^2 - s^2.

Thus:

h^2 = ( (s^2 - r^2 + 2r - 1) * ((r+1)^2 - s^2) ) / 4.

Now note that s^2 - r^2 + 2r - 1 = (s^2 - (r^2 - 2r + 1)) = s^2 - (r-1)^2. Because (r-1)^2 = r^2 -2r +1. So:

s^2 - r^2 + 2r - 1 = s^2 - (r^2 - 2r + 1) = s^2 - (r-1)^2.

Thus:

h^2 = ( (s^2 - (r-1)^2) * ((r+1)^2 - s^2) ) / 4.

Now we have a and h expressed. That's nice.

Now also compute p_x and p_y:

p_x = (1 + s - r)/2.

p_y = ( (r - 1 - s)(r + a) ) / (2 h). Since r + a = r + (1 + r^2 - s^2)/2 = (2r + 1 + r^2 - s^2)/2 = ((r+1)^2 - s^2)/2.

So r + a = ((r+1)^2 - s^2)/2. Then:

p_y = ( (r - 1 - s) * ((r+1)^2 - s^2)/2 ) / (2 h) = ( (r - 1 - s) * ((r+1)^2 - s^2) ) / (4 h).

Now note that (r - 1 - s) = -(s + 1 - r). So:

So the midpoint of AE is the foot of the perpendicular from M to AP. That's a known fact. So we can compute that midpoint.

Similarly, for Γ, the midpoint of AF is the foot from N to AP. So we can find coordinates of E and F by reflecting A across the foot? Because we can compute the foot of perpendicular from M to line AP. Then the midpoint of AE is that foot. So E = 2 * foot_M - A. Similarly, F = 2 * foot_N - A.

Usually for a circle, if a line intersects the circle at two points, then the perpendicular from the center to the line (the line segment) passes through the midpoint of the chord. That midpoint lies on the line connecting the center to the chord's midpoint. So indeed, the foot of perpendicular from M to the line AP is the midpoint of AE. However is it always true that the foot lies on the chord (i.e., between A and E)? Yes, because the chord is the segment of the line inside the circle; the midpoint is on the line and inside the segment, and the perpendicular from the center to the line passes through that midpoint. Let v = P - A. Then param point: A + t v. The foot X_M is the point on AP such that (X_M) · v = (A + t v) · v = A·v + t |v|^2, but also X_M is the projection of origin onto line, so X_M is the point on AP that minimizes distance to origin. That is the point where the vector from origin to point is perpendicular to direction v. So condition: (A + t v) ⟂ v. So (A + t v) · v = 0 → A·v + t |v|^2 = 0 → t = - (A·v) / |v|^2. Then X_M = A + t v. That's exactly the midpoint of AE (since A is on Ω, E is the second intersection). So indeed, the midpoint of AE is the foot from M to AP. So we can compute that.

Similarly, foot from N (1,0) to AP: param A + t v. Condition: ( (A + t v) - N ) ⟂ v. So (A - N + t v) · v = 0 → (A - N)·v + t |v|^2 = 0 → t = - ( (A - N)·v ) / |v|^2. Then foot_N = A + t v. That's midpoint of AF.

Maybe we can find a nicer expression for p_y using the derived expression (9): p_y = ((r - 1 - s)(r + a)) / (2 h). But also r + a = ((r+1)^2 - s^2)/2. So:

p_y = ( (r - 1 - s) * ((r+1)^2 - s^2) ) / (4 h). Since (r - 1 - s) = -(s + 1 - r). So:

p_y = - (s + 1 - r) * ((r+1)^2 - s^2) / (4 h). (10)

Now also h^2 = ((s^2 - (r-1)^2) * ((r+1)^2 - s^2) / 4. Let's denote:

U = (s^2 - (r-1)^2) = (s - (r-1))(s + (r-1)). That's positive.
V = ((r+1)^2 - s^2) = (r+1 - s)(r+1 + s). Positive.

Then h^2 = (U * V) / 4. So h = sqrt(U V) / 2.

Now p_y = - (s + 1 - r) * V / (4 h). Plug h: h = sqrt(U V) / 2. Then 4 h = 4 * sqrt(U V) / 2 = 2 sqrt(U V). So:

p_y = - (s + 1 - r) * V / (2 sqrt(U V)). Simplify: V / sqrt(V) = sqrt(V). So:

p_y = - (s + 1 - r) * sqrt(V) / (2 sqrt(U)). (11)

Thus p_y is expressed in terms of U and V. U = s^2 - (r-1)^2. V = (r+1)^2 - s^2. Also note that (s + 1 - r) = s - r + 1. That's something like ( (s - r) + 1). And V is (r+1)^2 - s^2.

p_x = (s - r + 1)/2.
p_y = - (s - r + 1) * sqrt(V) / (2 sqrt(U)). Actually check: (s + 1 - r) is s - r + 1. So p_y = - (s - r + 1) * sqrt(V) / (2 sqrt(U)). But note that sqrt(V) and sqrt(U) appear. So p_y is negative.

Let’s denote:

C_x = u - R.
D_x = v + S.

Then p_x = (C_x + D_x)/2.

We have:

(p_x)^2 + (p_y - 1)^2 = (p_x - C_x)^2 + p_y^2.

Expand:

p_x^2 + (p_y^2 - 2p_y + 1) = (p_x^2 - 2p_x C_x + C_x^2) + p_y^2.

Cancel p_y^2 both sides. Then:

p_x^2 - 2p_y + 1 = p_x^2 - 2p_x C_x + C_x^2.

Cancel p_x^2:

-2p_y + 1 = -2p_x C_x + C_x^2.

Thus:

-2p_y = -2p_x C_x + C_x^2 - 1.

Multiply by -1: 2p_y = 2p_x C_x - C_x^2 + 1.

So p_y = p_x C_x - (C_x^2)/2 + 1/2.

That's an expression. But we also have that P is equidistant to A and D, which would give another expression, but they should be consistent. But we can also derive using A and D, but it's okay.

We have A and E are points on Ω (circle centered at M) and the line AP is the line. The chord AE of Ω lies on AP. The midpoint of AE is the foot of the perpendicular from M to AP. Indeed, for a circle, the line from the center perpendicular to a chord bisects the chord. So the perpendicular from M to AP meets AP at the midpoint of AE. So that foot is exactly the midpoint. So indeed, X_M = foot_M is the midpoint of AE. So we can compute E = 2 X_M - A = A + 2 λ v.

Similarly, for N: foot_N = A + μ v, where μ = ((N - A) · v) / |v|^2. Then F = A + 2 μ v.

Thus we have:

E = A + 2 λ v, F = A + 2 μ v, with λ, μ as above.

However we derived a simplified expression for p_y in terms of u (which is m) and v (which is n) when we set R=1. That was because we scaled such that R=1. But we can also derive a general formula. Let's try to derive p_y in terms of m,n,h,R,S. Since A = (0,h), C = (m - R, 0), D = (n + S, 0). Then p_x = (m - R + n + S)/2.

Now p_y from A and C:

(p_x)^2 + (p_y - h)^2 = (p_x - (m - R))^2 + (p_y - 0)^2.

Simplify:

p_x^2 + (p_y^2 - 2p_y h + h^2) = (p_x^2 - 2p_x(m - R) + (m - R)^2) + p_y^2.

Cancel p_y^2 and p_x^2:

-2p_y h + h^2 = -2p_x(m - R) + (m - R)^2.

Thus:

2p_y h = 2p_x(m - R) - (m - R)^2 + h^2.

So p_y = [2p_x(m - R) - (m - R)^2 + h^2] / (2h). Similarly, using A and D gives:

p_y = [2p_x(n + S) - (n + S)^2 + h^2] / (2h).

Now we can simplify using relationships. But maybe we can find a nicer expression if we also use that m^2 + h^2 = R^2 and n^2 + h^2 = S^2. Then we can express (m - R)^2 = m^2 - 2mR + R^2. Wait, if circles intersect, there are no common internal tangents (the lines that touch both circles and intersect the segment joining centers). Actually for two intersecting circles, there are two common tangents (external) that do not intersect the segment joining centers, and there are no common internal tangents because the circles overlap. The internal center of similarity is the intersection of lines connecting points of tangency of common internal tangents, but those tangents exist only when circles are disjoint. So perhaps the internal center of similarity is not defined for intersecting circles? However there is still an internal center of similarity (the point that divides the segment joining centers internally in the ratio of radii) which is the homothety center for a homothety with negative ratio. That is often called the "internal center of similarity". For intersecting circles, does such a homothety map one circle to the other? Yes, if we take a homothety with center at that point and ratio -S/R, then the image of Ω (center M) is a circle with center at that point plus (-S/R) times (M - center), which will be N if the center is the internal division point. Let's verify: For two circles with centers M and N, radii R and S. The point X on MN such that XM / XN = R / S (internal division) is the internal center of similarity. Under homothety with center X and ratio -S/R (negative), the image of M is X + (-S/R)(M - X) = X - (S/R)(M - X). Since X divides MN internally, we can compute. For example, if M = 0, N = d, then X = (R/(R+S)) d. Then M - X = -X, so X - (S/R)(M - X) = X - (S/R)(-X) = X + (S/R)X = X(1 + S/R) = X ( (R+S)/R ) = (R/(R+S) d) * ((R+S)/R) = d = N. So indeed, N is the image of M under homothety with center X and ratio -S/R. Similarly, the image of Γ is Ω. So this homothety sends Ω to Γ with negative scale factor, and maps the intersection points A and B to each other? Let's see. Since A is on Ω, its image under this homothety is some point A' on Γ. Because A is on Ω, and the homothety sends Ω to Γ. So A' lies on Γ. But does A' equal B? Not necessarily. However note that A and B are symmetric with respect to MN. The line MN is the line of centers. The internal center X lies on MN. The homothety with center X and negative ratio is a composition of a rotation of 180° and a scaling. Let’s denote:

A = (0,0)
C = (C_x, C_y) = (m - R, h)
D = (D_x, D_y) = (n + S, h)

We have h = b/2.

Now, we can compute P as intersection of perpendicular bisectors. Let's derive formulas.

Since A is origin, the perpendicular bisector of AC is the set of points X such that |X - A| = |X - C| → |X|^2 = |X - C|^2. That gives:

X·X = (X - C)·(X - C) = X·X - 2 X·C + C·C → cancel X·X → 0 = -2 X·C + C·C → X·C = C·C / 2.

Thus, the perpendicular bisector of AC is the line (in 2D) given by X·C = |C|^2 / 2. That's a line (since C is a vector). Similarly, the perpendicular bisector of AD is X·D = |D|^2 / 2. The circumcenter P is the intersection of these two lines. Also, P must satisfy that P lies on perpendicular bisector of CD, which is x = (C_x + D_x)/2. However solving X·C = |C|^2/2 and X·D = |D|^2/2 is a linear system in X (two equations). That's nice. So we can solve for P.

Thus, we have:

P·C = (|C|^2)/2. (1)
P·D = (|D|^2)/2. (2)

Additionally, we know that C and D have the same y-coordinate. So we can compute P.

Now, we also have that A is at origin, so AP is just the vector P. So the direction of AP is P. So L is line through H parallel to P.

Now, H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. M = (m, h), N = (n, h). So MN is horizontal. So altitude from P is vertical. So H has x-coordinate equal to P_x. So H = (P_x, y_H). The altitude from M is perpendicular to PN. So we can compute y_H as before.

Now, we also need to compute E and F. Since A=0, line AP is the line through origin with direction P. So param: t P. Then E is the second intersection with Ω. Ω is circle centered at M with radius R. Since A is on Ω, we have |M| = R. The line through origin (A) with direction P intersects Ω at A (t=0) and another point E = t_E P, where t_E is determined by |t_E P - M|^2 = R^2. Since A is on Ω, we have |M|^2 = R^2. So:

|t P - M|^2 = R^2 → |t P|^2 - 2 t P·M + |M|^2 = R^2 → t^2 |P|^2 - 2 t (P·M) + R^2 = R^2 → t^2 |P|^2 - 2 t (P·M) = 0 → t (t |P|^2 - 2 (P·M)) = 0. Thus, t=0 (A) or t = 2 (P·M) / |P|^2. So E = (2 (P·M) / |P|^2) P.

Similarly, F is the second intersection with Γ, center N, radius S. Since A is on Γ, we have |N|^2 = S^2. Then |t P - N|^2 = S^2 → t^2 |P|^2 - 2 t (P·N) + S^2 = S^2 → t^2 |P|^2 - 2 t (P·N) = 0 → t = 0 or t = 2 (P·N) / |P|^2. So F = (2 (P·N) / |P|^2) P.

Thus, E and F are scalar multiples of P. That's nice.

Now, B = (0, b). So triangle BEF: B, E = α P, F = β P, where α = 2 (P·M) / |P|^2, β = 2 (P·N) / |P|^2.

Now, the circumcircle of BEF. Since E and F are collinear with origin, the line EF is the same as AP (through origin). So EF is a line through origin. B is a point not on that line generally. So the circle (BEF) is determined by B and the two points on a line through origin.

Now, the line L is through H parallel to P. So L is a line with direction P, passing through H.

We need to prove that L is tangent to circle (BEF). Perhaps we can find that the circle (BEF) is the circle with diameter something like the segment connecting the projections? But now we have a parametric expression.

Now, let's compute P in terms of m, n, b (and R,S). Since we have expressions for C and D.

We have:

C = (m - R, h) with h = b/2. D = (n + S, h). So C_x = m - R, D_x = n + S. Also, C_y = D_y = h.

Thus, C = (c, h), D = (d, h). Then:

|C|^2 = (m - R)^2 + h^2.
|D|^2 = (n + S)^2 + h^2.

Now, we also have R^2 = m^2 + h^2, S^2 = n^2 + h^2. So we can simplify expressions.

Let's compute C and D using these relations. Maybe we can find P as something like (some vector). Since we have linear equations:

P·C = |C|^2 / 2.
P·D = |D|^2 / 2.

This is a 2x2 linear system. Solve for P.

We can also note that P is the circumcenter, so it's the intersection of perpendicular bisectors. There might be a direct formula: P = (|C|^2 D - |D|^2 C) / (2 (C × D))? That's for the circumcenter in barycentric? Actually in vector form, the circumcenter of triangle with vertices A,B,C (A=0) can be expressed as (|B|^2 C - |C|^2 B) / (2 (B × C))? That's for 2D? That seems like something for the circumcenter of a triangle with one vertex at origin, using the fact that the perpendicular bisectors are lines. But we have two equations: P·C = |C|^2/2, P·D = |D|^2/2. That's a linear system. If we treat C and D as vectors, then we can solve for P as a linear combination of C and D? Actually we can represent P in the basis {C, D}. Since C and D are likely not parallel (they are not, because they have same y but different x, so they are not parallel unless they are the same vector). So they are linearly independent (unless one is a multiple of the other, which would happen if C and D are collinear with origin? That's not likely). So we can solve.

Write P = u C + v D. Then compute P·C = u |C|^2 + v (C·D). Similarly, P·D = u (C·D) + v |D|^2. This gives two equations:

u |C|^2 + v (C·D) = |C|^2 / 2.
u (C·D) + v |D|^2 = |D|^2 / 2.

We can solve for u and v. This seems doable.

Alternatively, we can directly compute P by solving the two linear equations. Since C and D are known, we can treat P as unknown (x,y) and solve:

C_x x + C_y y = (C_x^2 + C_y^2) / 2.
D_x x + D_y y = (D_x^2 + D_y^2) / 2.

That's a 2x2 linear system. That seems easier.

Let's denote:

C = (c1, c2) = (m - R, h).
D = (d1, d2) = (n + S, h).

Then:

c1 x + c2 y = (c1^2 + c2^2) / 2.   (1)
d1 x + d2 y = (d1^2 + d2^2) / 2.   (2)

We can solve for x,y.

Now, note that c2 = d2 = h. So the system is:

c1 x + h y = (c1^2 + h^2) / 2.
d1 x + h y = (d1^2 + h^2) / 2.

Subtract: (c1 - d1) x = (c1^2 - d1^2) / 2 = (c1 - d1)(c1 + d1) / 2. Since c1 - d1 ≠ 0 (likely), we get:

x = (c1 + d1) / 2.

Thus, P_x = (c1 + d1) / 2. That's the same as the midpoint of CD's x-coordinate, which we already knew because P lies on perpendicular bisector of CD (vertical). So that's consistent.

Now, we can plug x into one equation to find y:

c1 * ((c1 + d1)/2) + h y = (c1^2 + h^2) / 2 → (c1 (c1 + d1) ) / 2 + h y = (c1^2 + h^2) / 2 → multiply by 2: c1 (c1 + d1) + 2 h y = c1^2 + h^2 → c1^2 + c1 d1 + 2 h y = c1^2 + h^2 → cancel c1^2 → c1 d1 + 2 h y = h^2 → 2 h y = h^2 - c1 d1 → y = (h^2 - c1 d1) / (2 h).

Thus, P = ( (c1 + d1)/2, (h^2 - c1 d1) / (2 h) ).

Now, c1 = m - R, d1 = n + S.

Now, we also have expressions for R^2 = m^2 + h^2, S^2 = n^2 + h^2.

Now, we can compute P·M and P·N needed for α and β.

We have M = (m, h), N = (n, h). P is known. So we can compute P·M = P_x * m + P_y * h.

Similarly, P·N = P_x * n + P_y * h.

Now, we also need |P|^2.

This looks like a good path.

Now, we also need H, the orthocenter of triangle PMN. We have M and N on y = h. So MN is horizontal. So altitude from P is vertical. So H has x-coordinate equal to P_x. So H = (P_x, y_H). To find y_H, we can use altitude from M: line through M perpendicular to PN. Vector PN = N - P = (n - P_x, h - P_y). So direction perpendicular to PN is (h - P_y, n - P_x) or something. But perhaps we can derive a formula for y_H in terms of P, M, N. Since H is orthocenter, we have that H lies on the vertical line through P, and also on the line through M that is perpendicular to PN. That's okay.

Alternatively, we can compute H using the relation H = P + M + N - 2 * O, where O is circumcenter of PMN. But maybe easier to use altitudes. Since MN is horizontal, the altitude from P is vertical. So H's x-coordinate is P_x. The altitude from M is perpendicular to PN. So we need to find y such that point (P_x, y) lies on line through M with direction perpendicular to PN. So we can solve.

Let's compute H_y using that.

Given M = (m, h), N = (n, h). P = (p_x, p_y). Then:

PN = (n - p_x, h - p_y). A vector perpendicular to PN is (h - p_y, -(n - p_x)) or (h - p_y, p_x - n). Actually dot product: (n - p_x)*(h - p_y) + (h - p_y)*(p_x - n) = (n - p_x)*(h - p_y) - (n - p_x)*(h - p_y) = 0. So (h - p_y, p_x - n) is perpendicular. Also, (h - p_y, -(n - p_x)) works.

So the altitude from M has direction vector v_M = (h - p_y, p_x - n). Its param: (m, h) + t * (h - p_y, p_x - n). Intersection with x = p_x gives:

m + t (h - p_y) = p_x → t = (p_x - m) / (h - p_y), provided h ≠ p_y. Then y = h + t (p_x - n). So y_H = h + (p_x - m)(p_x - n) / (h - p_y). That's an expression.

But earlier we had a simpler expression when M was at (0,0) etc. This is messy. However note that we have p_y expressed in terms of h, m, R, S, etc. So maybe simplifications occur. But perhaps we can find H more directly using the fact that in any triangle, the reflection of the orthocenter over a side lies on the circumcircle. Since we have an orthocenter, we can also use the formula: H = A + B + C - 2 * O (circumcenter). But we don't know O.

Let's test numeric with this coordinate system.

Set A = (0,0). Choose b = 2 (so AB length 2). Then h = b/2 = 1. So MN is y = 1. Choose m = 0.5, n = 2 (ensuring u < v). Then compute R^2 = 0.5^2 + 1 = 1.25 → R ≈ 1.118. S^2 = 4 + 1 = 5 → S ≈ 2.236. Then C = (m - R, h) = (0.5 - 1.118, 1) = (-0.618, 1). D = (n + S, h) = (2 + 2.236, 1) = (4.236, 1). Then p_x = (c1 + d1)/2 = (-0.618 + 4.236)/2 = 3.618/2 = 1.809. p_y = (h^2 - c1 d1) / (2h) = (1 - (-0.618*4.236)) / 2 = (1 + 2.618) / 2 = 3.618/2 = 1.809. So P = (1.809, 1.809). That's interesting: P_y = p_y = 1.809, which is same as p_x? Actually p_x ≈ 1.809, p_y ≈ 1.809. So P is on the line y = x. That's plausible because we derived y = (h^2 - c1 d1)/(2h). With h=1, c1 d1 = (-0.618)*(4.236) ≈ -2.618, so h^2 - c1 d1 = 1 - (-2.618) = 3.618, /2 = 1.809. So indeed p_y = 1.809. So P = (1.809, 1.809). So P lies on line y = x.

Now, M = (0.5, 1). N = (2, 1). Compute α = 2 (P·M) / |P|^2. P·M = 1.809*0.5 + 1.809*1 = 0.9045 + 1.809 = 2.7135. |P|^2 = 1.809^2 + 1.809^2 = 2 * 3.273 = 6.546. So α = 2 * 2.7135 / 6.546 = 5.427 / 6.546 ≈ 0.8295. Then E = α P ≈ (1.809*0.8295, 1.809*0.8295) = (1.5, 1.5) approximately. Indeed earlier with A=0 we got E ≈ (1.5,1.5). That's consistent.

β = 2 (P·N) / |P|^2. P·N = 1.809*2 + 1.809*1 = 3.618 + 1.809 = 5.427. β = 2 * 5.427 / 6.546 = 10.854 / 6.546 ≈ 1.658. Then F = β P ≈ (1.809*1.658, 1.809*1.658) ≈ (3.0, 3.0) approximately (3.0, 3.0). So F ≈ (3,3). That's interesting.

(p_x - m)*(n - p_x) + (y - h)*(h - p_y) = 0.

We can solve for y. That might be simpler.

1.309 * 0.191 + (y - 1) * (-0.809) = 0 → 0.25 - 0.809(y - 1) = 0 → 0.809(y - 1) = 0.25 → y - 1 = 0.309 → y ≈ 1.309. So H ≈ (1.809, 1.309). That's interesting.

Now, L is line through H parallel to P, which is direction (1.809, 1.809). So L is line with slope 1 passing through (1.809, 1.309). So its equation: y - 1.309 = 1 * (x - 1.809). That's y = x - 0.5. So L is the line y = x - 0.5.

Now, the circle (BEF) we can compute its center and radius to see if L is tangent. Let's compute the circumcircle of B(0,2), E(1.5,1.5), F(3,3). We can compute its equation. Because E and F are on line y = x (since (1.5,1.5) and (3,3) satisfy y = x). B is (0,2). So the triangle is not isosceles but we can compute.

We can compute the circle's center O. Let's find perpendicular bisector of EF. EF is on y = x. Midpoint of EF is ((1.5+3)/2 = 2.25, (1.5+3)/2 = 2.25). So midpoint is (2.25,2.25). The perpendicular bisector of EF is the line perpendicular to y = x, i.e., slope -1, passing through (2.25,2.25). That's line: y - 2.25 = -1 (x - 2.25) → y = -x + 4.5. That's one line.

Now, perpendicular bisector of BE. B(0,2), E(1.5,1.5). Midpoint: (0.75, 1.75). Slope of BE: (1.5-2)/(1.5-0) = (-0.5)/1.5 = -1/3. So perpendicular slope = 3. Equation: y - 1.75 = 3 (x - 0.75). That's y = 3x - 2.25 + 1.75 = 3x - 0.5. So we solve with y = -x + 4.5.

Set -x + 4.5 = 3x - 0.5 → 4.5 + 0.5 = 3x + x → 5 = 4x → x = 1.25. Then y = -1.25 + 4.5 = 3.25. So O = (1.25, 3.25). That's center. Radius squared: distance from O to B: (0-1.25)^2 + (2-3.25)^2 = 1.5625 + 1.5625 = 3.125. So radius = sqrt(3.125) ≈ 1.768. Now, L is line y = x - 0.5. Distance from O(1.25,3.25) to L: line in form x - y - 0.5 = 0? Actually y = x - 0.5 → x - y - 0.5 = 0. Distance = |1.25 - 3.25 - 0.5| / sqrt(1^2 + (-1)^2) = | -2.5| / sqrt(2) = 2.5 / 1.414 ≈ 1.768. That's exactly the radius. So L is tangent. Great! So the analytic approach works with this coordinate system.

Given the numeric success, we can attempt to prove that L is tangent to (BEF) by showing that the distance from the center O to L equals the radius. We can compute O explicitly using vector algebra.

B = (0, b). h = b/2.

c1 = m - R

Thus, p_x = (c1 + d1)/2 = (m - R + n + S)/2.
p_y = (h^2 - c1 d1) / (2h) = (h^2 - (m - R)(n + S)) / (2h).

Now, we need to simplify expressions. Since R^2 = m^2 + h^2, S^2 = n^2 + h^2. So we can find rational expressions for R and S? But they appear linearly. (n + S) p_x + h p_y = ((n + S)^2 + h^2) / 2.   (2)

Subtract: ((m - R) - (n + S)) p_x = ( (m - R)^2 - (n + S)^2 ) / 2. The left coefficient is (m - n - R - S). That's messy. Better approach: Since we have a parametric description, we can compute the power of a point on L with respect to circle (BEF) and show that L is tangent because the distance from the center to L equals the radius. But we need an expression for the center O of (BEF). That seems plausible to compute by solving linear equations from equal distances. Let's attempt to do that.

Let’s denote:

Let’s define vectors:

Let B = (0, b). Let P be a vector (p_x, p_y). Then E = α P, F = β P, with α, β as above.

We want to find the circumcenter O of triangle BEF. Since B is not collinear with P, we can find O.

We have three points. Let’s denote O = (x, y). Then:

|O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2 = |O - F|^2.

We have:

|O - E|^2 = |O|^2 - 2 O·E + |E|^2.
|O - F|^2 = |O|^2 - 2 O·F + |F|^2.
|O - B|^2 = |O|^2 - 2 O·B + |B|^2.

Equality |O - E|^2 = |O - F|^2 yields:

-2 O·E + |E|^2 = -2 O·F + |F|^2 → 2 O·(F - E) = |F|^2 - |E|^2.

Since E and F are multiples of P, we have F - E = (β - α) P. So this is a linear equation in O. Similarly, equality |O - E|^2 = |O - B|^2 yields:

-2 O·E + |E|^2 = -2 O·B + |B|^2 → 2 O·(B - E) = |B|^2 - |E|^2.

So we have two linear equations in O. That's a system. Solve for O. That seems manageable. Then we can compute the distance from O to L (line through H parallel to P). L is defined by H + t P. So we need to show that the distance from O to L is equal to the radius (which is |O - B|). Alternatively, we can try to prove that L is the polar of something, but this is direct.

So plan:

1. Set up coordinates with A = (0,0). Then B = (0,b). Let M = (m, h), N = (n, h) with h = b/2.
2. Compute P (circumcenter of ACD) in terms of m,n,b.
3. Compute α, β.
4. Compute the circumcenter O of BEF using the linear equations.
5. Show that the distance from O to the line through H parallel to P equals |O - B|.
6. Conclude that L is tangent to (BEF).

But we also need H, the orthocenter of PMN. We'll compute H using altitude from P (vertical) and maybe a formula for y_H in terms of P, M, N. That seems messy, but perhaps we can also find H using the fact that in a triangle, the orthocenter can be expressed as H = A + B + C - 2 * O (circumcenter). However we don't know O. But we can compute H via altitudes: since MN is horizontal, H lies on vertical line x = P_x. So H = (P_x, y_H). We can find y_H by using that MH ⟂ PN. That gives an equation. That's okay. Then L is the line through H with direction P.

Now, we need to prove tangency. This is a computational proof, but we need to present it clearly. The algebra may be heavy, but we can simplify using the relationships. Let's try to derive expressions step by step.

First, note that we can scale such that b = 2 (so h = 1). Because we can apply a similarity transformation (scaling) to the whole configuration. However we must be careful: The problem statement is about circles with radii in a certain order; scaling changes the lengths, but the geometric property (tangency) is preserved. So we can assume without loss of generality that AB = 2 (i.e., b = 2). That simplifies expressions. Since we already have A=0, B=(0,2). Then h = 1.

Thus, we set b = 2. Then h = 1. So M = (m, 1), N = (n, 1). The circles intersect at A(0,0) and B(0,2). Then R^2 = m^2 + 1, S^2 = n^2 + 1. The ordering condition m < n (since C left of M and D right of N). That's fine.

Now, compute R and S: R = sqrt(m^2+1), S = sqrt(n^2+1). So they are square roots. But we can keep them symbolic.

Let's step back and consider the possibility of a synthetic proof. I recall a known geometry problem: "Let Ω and Γ be two intersecting circles with centers M and N. Let AB be their common chord. Let C be the point where Ω meets line MN on the opposite side of M from N, and D be the point where Γ meets line MN on the opposite side of N from M. Let P be the circumcenter of triangle ACD. Let H be the orthocenter of triangle PMN. Prove that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to the circumcircle of triangle BEF, where E = AP ∩ Ω (other than A), F = AP ∩ Γ (other than A)." That's exactly the problem. So it's a known configuration. The analytic solution is plausible, but we need to present it clearly.

Let's try to solve analytically with A=0, b=2, h=1. We'll compute P and then O. Then we'll compute distance from O to L. We'll also compute radius. That should simplify.

We have:

v = (v1, v2) = P.
M = (m, 1), N = (n, 1).

We have:

R^2 = m^2+1, S^2 = n^2+1.

c1 = m - R, d1 = n + S.
p_x = (c1+d1)/2.
p_y = (1 - c1 d1)/2.

Now, we also have that α = 2 (M·v) / |v|^2 = 2 (m v1 + v2) / A.
Similarly, β = 2 (n v1 + v2) / A.

Now, we derived that (α+β)/2 = ( (m+n) v1 + 2 v2 ) / A. That's a = (α+β)/2. So a A = (m+n) v1 + 2 v2.

Now, Eq1: v1 x + v2 y = a A.

But we also have from earlier that (α+β)/2 * |v|^2 = (m+n) v1 + 2 v2. That's a A. So indeed, Eq1 is v·O = a A.

Now, Eq2 from B and E: O·(B - α v) = (4 - α^2 A)/2 = 2 - 2 (M·v)^2 / A. That's fine.

Now, we can solve O. Let's do it systematically.

Let’s denote:

v = (v1, v2). A = v1^2+v2^2. B = M·v = m v1 + v2.

We have U = v·O = a A = (m+n) v1 + 2 v2. (1)

We also have V = O·(B - α v) = 2 - 2 B^2 / A. (2)

But we can also derive a linear equation for O in a different way: from equality |O - E|^2 = |O - B|^2, we got:

2 O·(B - E) = |B|^2 - |E|^2. That's what we used. That's linear.

Now, perhaps we can find O by solving two linear equations, but we already have a system. However note that we derived a formula for y: y = (V + B U) / A. That's the y-coordinate of O. So we have O = (x, y) with y known. Then we can compute O·w = b = ( -v2 x + v1 y ) / |v|.

We can also compute x from the system, but maybe we can avoid x by using a trick: The distance from O to L is | (O - H)·w |. H is on x = v1. So H = (v1, y_H). Then (O - H)·w = (O·w) - (H·w). So we need H·w. Compute H·w. Since w = (-v2, v1)/|v|, H·w = ( -v2 * v1 + v1 * y_H ) / |v| = ( -v1 v2 + v1 y_H ) / |v| = v1 ( y_H - v2 ) / |v|.

Thus, (O - H)·w = b - v1 ( y_H - v2 ) / |v|.

Now, b = ( -v2 x + v1 y ) / |v|. So we can compute b.

Maybe we can find that y_H is such that v1 ( y_H - v2 ) / |v| = something like b? That would make distance zero, not. So we need to compute.

But perhaps we can compute O·w directly from the linear system using Cramer's rule for the perpendicular component. Since we have two equations, we can solve for O in terms of u and w. Let's do that.

Let’s set up basis: Let e1 = v / |v| (unit parallel), e2 = w (unit perpendicular). Then O = a e1 + b e2, where a = (v·O)/|v| = U / |v|. Then b = O·w. We can also compute b by taking dot product of the second equation with w. Because the second equation is O·(B - α v) = RHS. So:

O·(B - α v) = (a e1 + b e2)·(B - α v) = a e1·(B - α v) + b e2·(B - α v). Since e1·v = |v|, e2·v = 0. So e1·(B - α v) = e1·B - α |v|. e2·(B - α v) = e2·B - α e2·v = e2·B.

Thus:

O·(B - α v) = a (B·e1 - α |v|) + b (B·e2). (Equation A)

Now, we also have a = U / |v|, and U is known. So we can solve for b.

Now, B·e1 = (0,2)·v / |v| = 2 v2 / |v|. B·e2 = (0,2)·w = 2 v1 / |v|. So:

a (B·e1 - α |v|) = (U / |v|) * (2 v2 / |v| - α |v|) = U * (2 v2 - α |v|^2) / |v|^2.

Thus, equation becomes:

U * (2 v2 - α A) / A + b * (2 v1 / |v|) = RHS.   (1)

But we can also compute α A = 2 (M·v) = 2 B. So α A = 2 B. So:

U * (2 v2 - 2 B) / A = 2 U (v2 - B) / A.

Now, U = a A = (m+n) v1 + 2 v2. So that's known. Also, B = m v1 + v2. So v2 - B = v2 - (m v1 + v2) = -m v1. So (v2 - B) = -m v1. Then U * (v2 - B) = U * (-m v1) = -m v1 U. So:

2 U (v2 - B) / A = -2 m v1 U / A.

Thus, the term becomes:

U * (2 v2 - α A) / A = -2 m v1 U / A.

Now, RHS = 2 - 2 B^2 / A.

So we have:

-2 m v1 U / A + b * (2 v1 / |v|) = 2 - 2 B^2 / A.

Multiply by A: -2 m v1 U + b * (2 v1 A / |v|) = 2A - 2 B^2.

Divide by 2: - m v1 U + b * (v1 A / |v|) = A - B^2.

Now, note that v1 A / |v| = v1 * |v|. Because A = |v|^2, so A / |v| = |v|. So:

b * (v1 |v|) = A - B^2 + m v1 U.

Thus,

b = (A - B^2 + m v1 U) / (v1 |v|).   (Equation for b)

Now, U = (m+n) v1 + 2 v2. So that's okay.

Now, we also need H·w. H = (v1, y_H). So H·w = ( -v2 * v1 + v1 * y_H ) / |v| = v1 ( y_H - v2 ) / |v|.

We can compute y_H from orthocenter. Let's derive y_H.

We have triangle PMN. M = (m,1), N = (n,1). P = (v1, v2). H lies on x = v1. So H = (v1, y_H). Orthocenter condition: (H - M) · (N - P) = 0. That's:

(v1 - m, y_H - 1) · (n - v1, 1 - v2) = 0.

Thus:

(v1 - m)*(n - v1) + (y_H - 1)*(1 - v2) = 0 → (y_H - 1)*(1 - v2) = - (v1 - m)*(n - v1). So:

y_H - 1 = - (v1 - m)*(n - v1) / (1 - v2), provided v2 ≠ 1. If v2 = 1, then P is on the line y = 1 (i.e., on MN), which would be degenerate? But P could be on MN? Let's check. P is circumcenter of ACD. Could it be on MN? Possibly if A, C, D are such that P lies on MN. But earlier we argued that P is on perpendicular bisector of CD, which is vertical. MN is horizontal, so P is not on MN unless it's at the intersection (which is the midpoint of CD). That point lies on MN? The midpoint of CD has y-coordinate 1 (since C and D have y=1). So its y-coordinate is 1. The x-coordinate is p_x. So that point lies on MN? MN is the line y = 1, yes. So P_x (p_x) is the x-coordinate of the midpoint, which lies on MN (y=1). But P is (p_x, p_y). p_y is not necessarily 1. So P is not on MN (unless p_y = 1). But is it possible that p_y = 1? That would make P on MN. Let's see if that can happen. With b=2, h=1, p_y = (1 - (m - R)(n + S))/2. That could be 1. That would imply (1 - (m - R)(n + S))/2 = 1 → 1 - (m - R)(n + S) = 2 → -(m - R)(n + S) = 1 → (m - R)(n + S) = -1. That seems plausible. So P could be on MN. In that case, v2 = 1. Then the altitude from P is vertical, and H lies on that vertical line. But also, H is orthocenter, but if P is on MN, then triangle PMN has a right angle at something? Actually if P is on MN, then P is collinear with M and N, making triangle degenerate? No, P is on the line MN? If v2 = 1, then P lies on y = 1, which is MN. So P is on MN. Then triangle PMN has all three points on the same line? That would be degenerate. But M and N are on y = 1. If P is also on y = 1, then they are collinear. The problem states that H is orthocenter of triangle PMN, which would be undefined if triangle is degenerate. Therefore, we must have that triangle PMN is non-degenerate. This implies that P is not on the line MN. So v2 ≠ 1. Similarly, we should check that P is not on MN. Indeed, the problem defines H as orthocentre of triangle PMN, so triangle PMN is non-degenerate. That means P is not on MN. So v2 ≠ 1. That's consistent with our earlier argument that P is not on MN. So we can assume v2 ≠ 1. Good.

Thus, we can compute y_H as:

y_H = 1 - (v1 - m)*(n - v1) / (1 - v2). That's okay.

Now, H·w = v1 ( y_H - v2 ) / |v|. So:

H·w = v1 ( 1 - (v1 - m)*(n - v1) / (1 - v2) - v2 ) / |v| = v1 ( 1 - v2 - (v1 - m)*(n - v1) / (1 - v2) ) / |v|.

Simplify.

Now, b = (A - B^2 + m v1 U) / (v1 |v|). Then (O - H)·w = b - H·w.

We want to show that |(O - H)·w| = |O - B|.

But maybe there is a more clever way: Instead of solving for O, we can directly prove that the line L is tangent by showing that the power of H with respect to circle (BEF) is equal to the square of the distance from H to the point where L touches, but we don't know that point. However we could try to prove that L is the polar of B with respect to the circle (ACD) or something, and then use some property of the orthocenter to relate to circle BEF.

Let's explore the possibility of using the concept of "Euler line" and "nine-point circle". In triangle PMN, the nine-point circle passes through the midpoints of the sides, the feet of the altitudes, and the midpoints of the segments from orthocenter to vertices. The circle (BEF) might be the nine-point circle of triangle PMN. Let's test numeric with the first example (m=0.5,n=2). Compute midpoints of PMN: P(1.809,1.809), M(0.5,1), N(2,1). Midpoints: PM: ((1.809+0.5)/2=1.1545, (1.809+1)/2=1.4045). PN: ((1.809+2)/2=1.9045, (1.809+1)/2=1.4045). MN: ((0.5+2)/2=1.25, (1+1)/2=1). The circumcenter of PMN is somewhere. We can compute quickly. But we saw that O_BEF = (1.25,3.25). That's not the nine-point center of PMN. So not that.

Maybe (BEF) is the circle with diameter BH? Not.

Let's try a synthetic approach: Consider the homothety centered at A that sends Ω to Γ. That's not a homothety because A is on both circles, but a homothety with center A mapping Ω to Γ would need to send M to N and radius R to S. That's a homothety with ratio S/R. For this to be N, we need N = A + (S/R)(M - A). That's a condition that may not hold. However we can consider the homothety that sends Ω to Γ with center at the external center of similarity, which is on MN. That's a different transformation. So not that.

λ v2 + μ (A - B v2) = v1.   (II)

We can solve for λ and μ. This seems messy. But note that we can also compute b = O·w = ( -v2 x + v1 y ) / |v|. So we need to find an expression for -v2 x + v1 y in terms of U, V, and v. Let's compute y_H in a more symmetric way, maybe relating to something like (v2 - 1) etc. We have:

Thus, (O - H)·w = b - H·w. This seems messy.

Now, the line AP (through origin) will intersect Ω at A and another point E = t_E P, where t_E = 2 (M·P) / |P|^2. That's fine. Similarly for F.

Now, B is (d,0). So we have triangle BEF. The circle (BEF). The line L is through H (orthocenter of PMN) parallel to P. Since MN is vertical, the altitude from P is horizontal. That's simpler! Because MN is vertical. So the altitude from P is horizontal (perpendicular to MN). So H has y-coordinate equal to p_y (the y-coordinate of P). Since P is not on MN (triangle non-degenerate), p_y is something. The altitude from M is perpendicular to PN, etc. That might be easier to compute.

Now, the line L is parallel to P. So L is a line with direction P. That's okay.
