## Solution
Let $\Omega$ and $\Gamma$ be circles with centres $M$ and $N$, respectively, such that the radius of $\Omega$ is less than the radius of $\Gamma$.  Suppose they intersect at two distinct points $A$ and $B$.  The line $MN$ meets $\Omega$ at $C$ and $\Gamma$ at $D$ with $C$, $M$, $N$, $D$ lying on $MN$ in that order.  $P$ is the circumcentre of $\triangle ACD$.  The line $AP$ meets $\Omega$ again at $E\neq A$ and $\Gamma$ again at $F\neq A$.  $H$ is the orthocentre of $\triangle PMN$.  Prove that the line through $H$ parallel to $AP$ is tangent to the circumcircle of $\triangle BEF$.

---

### 1.  Setting up coordinates
We apply a similarity transformation (translation, rotation, scaling) so that  
\[
A=(0,0),\qquad B=(2,0).
\]
(We may take $AB=2$ because similarity preserves tangency and the given order of points.)
The common chord $AB$ is horizontal; therefore the line joining the centres $M$ and $N$ is vertical (it is the perpendicular bisector of $AB$).  The midpoint of $AB$ is $(1,0)$, so $MN$ passes through $(1,0)$ and is vertical.  Consequently we can write
\[
M=(1,y_M),\qquad N=(1,y_N)
\]
for some real numbers $y_M$, $y_N$.  The circles $\Omega$ and $\Gamma$ have radii
\[
R=MA,\qquad S=NA.
\]
Because $A$ and $B$ lie on both circles,
\[
|M|^2 = 1^2 + y_M^2 = R^2,\qquad |N|^2 = 1^2 + y_N^2 = S^2.
\]
Thus
\[
y_M^2 = R^2-1,\qquad y_N^2 = S^2-1.
\]
(The fact that $R<S$ implies $|y_M|<|y_N|$; we will see later that the ordering $C,M,N,D$ forces $y_M>y_N$.)

The line $MN$ (the vertical line $x=1$) intersects $\Omega$ at two points:
\[
C_1=(1,y_M+R),\quad C_2=(1,y_M-R).
\]
The problem states that $C$ and $M$ and $N$ and $D$ lie on $MN$ in that order, so $C$ is the point of $\Omega$ on the same side of $M$ as the direction from $M$ away from $N$; with $M$ above $N$ (as will be justified) this is the upper intersection.  Similarly, $D$ is the point of $\Gamma$ on the opposite side of $N$ from $M$; with $M$ above $N$ this is the lower intersection.  Therefore we have
\[
C=(1,y_M+R),\qquad D=(1,y_N-S).
\]

### 2.  The circumcentre $P$ of $\triangle ACD$
We have $A=(0,0)$, $C=(1,y_C)$ with $y_C=y_M+R$, $D=(1,y_D)$ with $y_D=y_N-S$.  The segment $CD$ is vertical, so its perpendicular bisector is horizontal; hence $P$ has the same $y$‑coordinate as the midpoint of $CD$:
\[
p_y = \frac{y_C+y_D}{2}.
\]

The perpendicular bisector of $AC$ is the set of points $X$ with $|X|=|X-C|$.  Because $A$ is the origin, this condition simplifies to $X\cdot C = \frac12 |C|^2$.  For $P$ we also have $P\cdot D = \frac12 |D|^2$.  Using $C=(1,y_C)$ and $D=(1,y_D)$ we obtain
\[
\begin{cases}
p_x + y_C p_y = \frac12(1^2+y_C^2),\\[2mm]
p_x + y_D p_y = \frac12(1^2+y_D^2).
\end{cases}
\]

Subtracting the two equations gives $(y_C-y_D)p_y = \frac12(y_C^2-y_D^2) = \frac12(y_C-y_D)(y_C+y_D)$, so $p_y = \frac12(y_C+y_D)$, which is already known.  Substituting $p_y$ into the first equation yields
\[
p_x = \frac12(1+y_C^2) - y_C\cdot\frac12(y_C+y_D) = \frac12(1+y_C^2 - y_C^2 - y_C y_D) = \frac12(1 - y_C y_D).
\]

Thus
\[
P = \Bigl(\frac12(1 - y_C y_D),\;\frac12(y_C+y_D)\Bigr).
\]

Now recall that $y_C = y_M+R$, $y_D = y_N-S$.  The expressions $R$ and $S$ are related to $y_M$, $y_N$ via $R^2 = 1 + y_M^2$, $S^2 = 1 + y_N^2$.  We shall keep $R$, $S$ symbolic for now; they will simplify later.

### 3.  The orthocentre $H$ of $\triangle PMN$
$M=(1,y_M)$, $N=(1,y_N)$, $P=(p_x,p_y)$ with $p_x = \frac12(1 - y_C y_D)$, $p_y = \frac12(y_C+y_D)$.  The segment $MN$ is vertical, so the altitude from $P$ is horizontal; therefore $H$ has the same $y$‑coordinate as $P$, i.e.
\[
H = (h_x,\; p_y)
\]
for some $h_x$ (the altitude from $M$ or $N$ will determine $h_x$).  Because $H$ is the orthocentre, we have $(H-M)\perp (N-P)$.  Compute
\[
N-P = (1-p_x,\; y_N-p_y),\qquad H-M = (h_x-1,\; p_y-y_M).
\]

The orthogonality condition $(H-M)\cdot (N-P)=0$ gives
\[
(h_x-1)(1-p_x) + (p_y-y_M)(y_N-p_y) = 0.
\]

We need $h_x$ (the $x$‑coordinate of $H$) to write the line through $H$ parallel to $AP$.  Since $AP$ passes through the origin, its direction is simply $P$; hence the line $L$ through $H$ parallel to $AP$ is
\[
L:\;(x,y) = H + t\,P.
\]

Thus $L$ is determined by $P$ and $H$, so we only need to prove that $L$ is tangent to the circle $(BEF)$.

### 4.  The points $E$ and $F$
$E$ is the second intersection of line $AP$ (through $A$ and $P$) with $\Omega$.  Because $A$ is the origin, any point on $AP$ is $tP$, $t\in\mathbb{R}$.  The circle $\Omega$ has centre $M$ and radius $R$, and $A$ lies on it, so $|M|=R$.  The condition $|tP - M|^2 = R^2$ yields
\[
t^2|P|^2 - 2t\,(P\cdot M) + |M|^2 = R^2 = |M|^2,
\]
hence $t\bigl(t|P|^2 - 2(P\cdot M)\bigr)=0$.  The non‑zero solution is
\[
t_E = \frac{2(P\cdot M)}{|P|^2}.
\]
Thus
\[
E = \frac{2(P\cdot M)}{|P|^2}\,P.
\]

Similarly, $F$ is the second intersection of $AP$ with $\Gamma$:
\[
F = \frac{2(P\cdot N)}{|P|^2}\,P.
\]

Notice that $P\cdot M$ and $P\cdot N$ are scalar products; they involve $y_M$, $y_N$ and $p_x$, $p_y$.  Later we will simplify them using the expressions for $y_C$ and $y_D$.

### 5.  The circle $(BEF)$ and its centre
$B=(2,0)$.  Let us denote
\[
\alpha = \frac{2(P\cdot M)}{|P|^2},\qquad \beta = \frac{2(P\cdot N)}{|P|^2},
\]
so $E=\alpha P$, $F=\beta P$.  The three points $B$, $E$, $F$ are not collinear (unless something degenerate happens).  The circumcentre $O$ of $\triangle BEF$ is the intersection of the perpendicular bisectors of $BE$ and $BF$.  Because $E$ and $F$ are collinear with the origin, the chord $EF$ lies on the line $AP$; its perpendicular bisector is perpendicular to $P$.  Thus $O$ can be expressed in the basis of $P$ and a vector $Q$ perpendicular to $P$ (say $Q$ is obtained by rotating $P$ by $90^\circ$).  A convenient way is to solve the linear system obtained from the equalities $|O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2$ and $|O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2$; we already know from the perpendicular bisector of $EF$ that $O\cdot P = \frac12(|E|^2 + |F|^2 - |E-F|^2)/?$ Actually let's derive directly.

The equality $|O-E|^2 = |O-B|^2$ gives
\[
2 O\cdot (B-E) = |B|^2 - |E|^2. \tag{1}
\]
Similarly $|O-F|^2 = |O-B|^2$ gives
\[
2 O\cdot (B-F) = |B|^2 - |F|^2. \tag{2}
\]

Since $E=\alpha P$ and $F=\beta P$, we have
\[
|E|^2 = \alpha^2|P|^2,\quad |F|^2 = \beta^2|P|^2,\quad |B|^2 = 4.
\]

Moreover $B-E = (2- \alpha p_x,\; 0- \alpha p_y)$, and $B-F$ is similar.

The system (1),(2) is linear in $O$; solving it gives $O$ explicitly.  However we can find a simpler expression for the distance from $O$ to $L$ by noticing that $L$ is parallel to $P$ and passes through $H$.  The distance from $O$ to $L$ is the length of the component of $O-H$ perpendicular to $P$.  So we need to compute $|(O-H)\cdot Q|$ where $Q$ is a unit vector perpendicular to $P$.  The radius $R$ of $(BEF)$ is $|O-B|$.  The tangency condition is therefore
\[
|(O-H)\cdot Q| = |O-B|.
\]

Now $O$ can be expressed as
\[
O = a P + b Q,
\]
where $a = \frac{O\cdot P}{|P|^2}$ (the parallel component).  From the perpendicular bisector of $EF$ we have
\[
O\cdot P = \frac12(|E|^2 + |F|^2 - |E-F|^2) = \frac12(\alpha^2|P|^2 + \beta^2|P|^2 - (\beta-\alpha)^2|P|^2) = \frac12(\alpha^2+\beta^2 - (\beta-\alpha)^2)|P|^2 = \frac12(2\alpha\beta)|P|^2 = \alpha\beta|P|^2.
\]
Wait check: $|E-F|^2 = (\alpha-\beta)^2|P|^2$. Then $|E|^2+|F|^2 - |E-F|^2 = \alpha^2|P|^2 + \beta^2|P|^2 - (\alpha-\beta)^2|P|^2 = (\alpha^2+\beta^2 - (\alpha^2-2\alpha\beta+\beta^2))|P|^2 = 2\alpha\beta|P|^2$. Thus $O\cdot P = \alpha\beta|P|^2$.  So $a = \alpha\beta|P|^2 / |P|^2 = \alpha\beta$.  That's interesting.  But earlier we derived $U = v\cdot O$ from the equation with $C$ and $D$ for the circumcenter $P$? Wait $P$ is circumcenter, not $O$.  Here $O$ is circumcenter of $BEF$.  So we have $O\cdot P = \alpha\beta|P|^2$.

But we need $O$ expressed in terms of $P$ and $Q$.  So $O = \alpha\beta P + b Q$ where $b = O\cdot Q$ (since $Q$ is unit).  Then $|O-B|^2 = |(\alpha\beta P + b Q) - B|^2$.

Now $H$ is $(h_x, p_y)$.  We'll compute $h_x$ later.  The line $L$ is $H + t P$.  The distance from $O$ to $L$ is $|(O-H)\cdot Q| = |b - H\cdot Q|$.  So we need to prove $|b - H\cdot Q| = |O-B|$.

This seems still messy.  However maybe we can find that $b = H\cdot Q$ and $|O-B| = 0$? Not.

Let's take a step back.  The coordinate system with $A=(0,0)$, $B=(2,0)$, $MN$ vertical, $C$ and $D$ on $x=1$, gave us simple expressions for $P$ and also allowed us to compute $H$ more directly.  Let's compute $H$ using the orthocenter conditions.

We have $M=(1,y_M)$, $N=(1,y_N)$.  $P$ is known.  The triangle $PMN$ is not degenerate; $MN$ is vertical.  The altitude from $P$ is horizontal.  So $H$ lies on the horizontal line $y = p_y$.  Let $H = (h_x, p_y)$.  The altitude from $M$ is perpendicular to $PN$.  So $(H-M) \perp (N-P)$.  Similarly, $(H-N) \perp (M-P)$.  We'll use one.

Now, compute $N-P = (1-p_x, y_N-p_y)$.  $H-M = (h_x-1, p_y - y_M)$.  The orthogonality condition gives
\[
(h_x-1)(1-p_x) + (p_y - y_M)(y_N - p_y) = 0.
\]

Thus
\[
h_x = 1 - \frac{(p_y - y_M)(y_N - p_y)}{1-p_x}. \tag{3}
\]

We also have $p_x = \frac12(1 - y_C y_D)$, $p_y = \frac12(y_C+y_D)$, and $y_C = y_M+R$, $y_D = y_N-S$.  So we can substitute.

But perhaps there is a more clever way to avoid computing $h_x$: The line $L$ through $H$ parallel to $AP$ is $L: H + t P$.  Its direction is $P$.  If we can show that $L$ is tangent to $(BEF)$, then $L$ is the line through $H$ with direction $P$.  So we need to prove that the distance from $O$ to $L$ equals $|O-B|$.  That's a condition.  Perhaps we can find an explicit expression for $O$ in terms of $P$ and $B$, and then compute the distance.

Let's try to solve for $O$ from the linear system (1),(2).  Because $E$ and $F$ are scalar multiples of $P$, the system becomes:
\[
\begin{cases}
2 O\cdot (B - \alpha P) = 4 - \alpha^2 |P|^2,\\
2 O\cdot (B - \beta P) = 4 - \beta^2 |P|^2.
\end{cases}
\]

We can solve for $O$ as a linear combination of $P$ and $B$ maybe.  But maybe there is a known formula: The circumcentre of a triangle with vertices $B$, $E$, $F$ where $E$ and $F$ are collinear with $A$ (origin) can be expressed in terms of $B$ and $P$.  Let's attempt to find $O$ by solving the system.

Let’s denote $v = P$.  Then $E = \alpha v$, $F = \beta v$.  The equalities $|O - B|^2 = |O - \alpha v|^2$ and $|O - B|^2 = |O - \beta v|^2$ lead to:

\[
2 O\cdot (B - \alpha v) = |B|^2 - |\alpha v|^2 = 4 - \alpha^2 |v|^2.
\]
\[
2 O\cdot (B - \beta v) = 4 - \beta^2 |v|^2.
\]

These are two linear equations in $O$.  The system can be written as:

\[
\begin{cases}
2 O\cdot B - 2\alpha (O\cdot v) = 4 - \alpha^2 |v|^2,\\
2 O\cdot B - 2\beta (O\cdot v) = 4 - \beta^2 |v|^2.
\end{cases}
\]

Subtract the two equations: $2(\beta-\alpha)(O\cdot v) = (\beta^2 - \alpha^2)|v|^2 = (\beta-\alpha)(\beta+\alpha)|v|^2$.  Assuming $\beta \neq \alpha$ (which holds because $R < S$ implies $P\cdot M \neq P\cdot N$? Actually we can check later).  Then we get:

\[
2(O\cdot v) = (\alpha+\beta)|v|^2.
\]

Thus $O\cdot v = \frac{\alpha+\beta}{2} |v|^2$.  But earlier we derived $O\cdot v = \alpha\beta |v|^2$ from the perpendicular bisector of $EF$.  Which is correct?  Let's recompute:

We have $E = \alpha v$, $F = \beta v$.  The perpendicular bisector of $EF$ consists of points $X$ such that $(X - \frac{\alpha+\beta}{2} v) \cdot v = 0$, i.e., $X\cdot v = \frac{\alpha+\beta}{2} |v|^2$.  That's correct.  So $O$ must satisfy $O\cdot v = \frac{\alpha+\beta}{2} |v|^2$.  Meanwhile the equality $|O - E|^2 = |O - B|^2$ gave $2 O\cdot (B - E) = |B|^2 - |E|^2$.  That's a different linear equation.  The two equations are not independent; they determine $O$.  So both must hold.  But we derived $O\cdot v = \frac{\alpha+\beta}{2} |v|^2$ from the perpendicular bisector of $EF$ (which is a consequence of $E$ and $F$ being on the circle with centre $O$).  That's valid.  Then from the other equation we can find $O$'s component perpendicular to $v$.  So we have $O = a v + b w$ with $a = \frac{\alpha+\beta}{2}$ (since $O\cdot v = a |v|^2$).  Then $b$ is determined by the second equation.

Now, the equation from $B$ and $E$ gives:
\[
2 O\cdot (B - \alpha v) = 4 - \alpha^2 |v|^2.
\]
Plug $O = a v + b w$, $a = \frac{\alpha+\beta}{2}$, $w \perp v$.  Then $O\cdot (B - \alpha v) = a v\cdot (B - \alpha v) + b w\cdot (B - \alpha v) = a (v\cdot B - \alpha |v|^2) + b (w\cdot B)$ because $v\cdot w = 0$.  So:

\[
2[ a (v\cdot B - \alpha |v|^2) + b (w\cdot B) ] = 4 - \alpha^2 |v|^2.
\]

Now $v\cdot B = p_x * 0? Actually B = (2,0)$, $v = (p_x,p_y)$. So $v\cdot B = 2 p_x$.  Also $|v|^2 = p_x^2+p_y^2$.  $w$ is a unit vector perpendicular to $v$, say $w = (-p_y, p_x) / |v|$. Then $w\cdot B = ( -p_y * 2 + p_x * 0 ) / |v| = -2 p_y / |v|$.  That's a bit messy.

But we can also use the equation from $B$ and $F$ to get a symmetric expression.  However maybe there is a better approach: Since $O$ is the circumcenter of $BEF$, we can compute $O$ using the formula for the circumcenter of a triangle given coordinates.  That's a standard computation, but we already have expressions for $P
